9/20/2007 4:21:14 AM

Okay, let's wade into this one.

Gene Ward Smith wrote:
> --- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, "George D. Secor" <gdsecor@...> wrote:
> >>How about "temperament family"? > > Bad for four reasons:
> > (1) It encourages you to think of it as a family, and not a singular > entity.

A family is a singular entity.

> (2) It suggests the tuning is more important, which is wrong.

It suggests nothing of the sort. It does suggest that we'll explain the term "temperament" first, which makes sense to me. Go from tuning to temperament to temperament family/class with increasing abstraction.

> (3) We are already using the term to mean something else.

Bingo!

> (4) It's two words. Since the idea is important, that's one too many.

For now two words will have to do. I bet you won't use them as often as you think you will.

I'll add one:

(5) Last time round, we settled on "temperament class".

> However, I suppose a new term would give me license to define things > algebraically in a way I would prefer.

Think a nice one and make use of it in a significant publication.

>>My main concern is that the term "meantone temperament" (without > any >>qualifiers) *not be used* (ever!) to refer to anything other than a >>specific (1/4-comma) tempered tuning.
> > Why? Should miracle temperament only refer to the offical size of > secor?

Ah, but "miracle temperament" can't refer to a singular entity! That'd be "the miracle temperament" or "Miracle Temperament". Without the article, "miracle temperament" is an uncountable noun, and can easily refer to a set of temperaments. At a stretch, it can also refer to a single temperament, if you think of it as an abstract concept that only takes on a real form when set on a musical instrument.

Otherwise, what temperament is Barbour referring to in "Tuning and Temperament"?

I can see an argument for saying "miracle temperaments" instead for disambiguation. Of course you won't like that because it really does put tunings first.

> The tail wags the dog.

In this case, I don't think the world in general wants to remember what tuning "the miracle temperament" has. But "the meantone temperament" is relatively clear.

> If you want to use "family", I suggest just that word, and leave > off "temperament". Then it becomes "meantone" or "meantone > family", "miracle" or "miracle family", and so forth, but > never "meantone temperament family" (UGH!) But better would be a > different coinage--call it a "regulum", say. "Meantone > regulum", "miracle regulum", with "meantone" or "miracle" for short.

That works as an abbreviation. But you'd need some way to specify what kind of family you're referring to.

Graham

ðŸ”—Graham Breed <gbreed@gmail.com>

9/20/2007 4:44:26 AM

Tom Dent wrote:
> I missed the part where anyone beyond GWS agreed that a temperament
> should be an abstract mapping from rational numbers to a 'group of
> smaller rank' - ie a mathematical construction. (What are temperament
> ordinaire and irregular circulating temperament, then? Must they be
> rechristened 'tuning ordinaire' etc.)

Whether you think of them as mappings is neither here nor there. They are mappings and whatever properties you think temperaments should have are consistent with these mappings. Irregular temperaments included.

> If I ask how this or that piece sounds in '2/7 comma meantone
> (temperament)', none of the words means a mathematical construct. They
> mean that someone tuned an (unspecified) instrument with some
> (unspecified) pitch standard by narrowing each fifth, or widening
> fourths, etc., by some particular amount ... or someone programmed a
> tone-producing computer! ... in order to render certain harmonic
> progressions tolerable. Some features of the audible result will be
> very well approximated by mathematical entities (and hopefully we can
> see beyond the ends of our noses that inaudible inaccuracies in tuning
> are ignorable) but what I mean is the audible result.

How do you interpret "2/7" as not being a mathematical construct?

> If one wants to be more precise, a temperament is a way to tune
> musical intervals where at least some intervals are adjusted away from
> purity by an irrational amount. Even without a very accurate
> mathematical description of what this amount is (eg temperament
> ordinaire!) one can have a temperament.

Why does it have to be irrational and, without a very accurate mathematical description, how do you know that it's irrational?

> This group is 'tuning' not 'tuning-math'. If someone wants to talk
> about relevant mathematics they are welcome, but it does no good to
> complain that other people are using words in the traditional and
> practical sense of audible procedures and results in tuning. Oh, come off it.

Graham

ðŸ”—Tom Dent <stringph@gmail.com>

9/20/2007 5:14:04 AM

I missed this message the first time round. It brings up a possibly
interesting question. According to the group-theoretical definition of
meantone, it can equally well be tuned with fifths (say) 1/18 comma
narrower than pure, as with (say) 1/9 comma.

Also according to what Monz says below, about 'tempering out the
syntonic comma', 1/18-comma should equally well be a member in good
standing of the meantone class. Making the fifths narrow by 1/18-comma
is, logically, a way of tempering out 81/80.

So can a regular tuning with 1/18-comma narrowed fifths possibly be
meantone, or not, and then why not? (I would argue yes, but only under
the widest possible meaning of the term.)

~~~T~~~

ðŸ”—Graham Breed <gbreed@gmail.com>

9/20/2007 5:27:03 AM

Carl Lumma wrote:
> --- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, "Tom Dent" <stringph@...> wrote:
> >>I missed the part where anyone beyond GWS agreed that a temperament
>>should be an abstract mapping from rational numbers to a 'group of
>>smaller rank' - ie a mathematical construction.
> > > You must not be reading my posts, and I think Paul Erlich and
> Herman Miller agree too. Graham has used the term that way
> for years, but now it seems he doesn't agree (or something).

I've always objected to Gene's definitions, in so far as I've understood them. That's the price you pay for trying to be precise. In this case I don't agree that the mapping should be from rational numbers. For example, you may remember Tony said that we were looking at a tuning which approximated the intervals in the timbre of his bellophone. I'd be happy to call that a temperament. (As it happens, he's using the equivalent of Pythagorean intonation for the timbre.) For now I call the things we map from "just intonation". That'd cause all kinds of trouble if people understood what I meant by it. <wink>

One thing you left out is the distinction between "into" and "onto" which is the same as "homomorphism" or "epimorphism". But let's leave that for tuning-math, eh?

>>(What are temperament
>>ordinaire and irregular circulating temperament, then? Must they be
>>rechristened 'tuning ordinaire' etc.)
> > Strictly speaking, "temperament" in the above sense is short
> for "regular temperament". But anyway, words to not have
> one and only one meaning for all time. They evolve, and take
> on specialized meanings in specialize contexts.

Well, that depends. If the group being mapped to is a set of intervals, measured in cents, then it has to be a regular temperament. And it really is a temperament in the sense of having a tuning. (But not in the sense of A440 being a tuning.)

Alternatively, you can interpret the group being mapped to as a set of, let's say, "abstract intervals". By that I mean a set of intervals measured between notes. For example, 5:4 maps to 4 steps on a 12 note scale. You then have a separate mapping (not a homomorophism) from notes to pitches which allows for steps being of different sizes. This definition is fine for irregular temperaments. But to define a temperament you need that mapping from notes to pitches (a tuning). Otherwise it's a temperament class (neither regular nor irregular).

My objection to Gene's definition as I remember it is that he doesn't specify what this group you map to consists of.

Graham

ðŸ”—Graham Breed <gbreed@gmail.com>

9/20/2007 6:17:29 AM

Tom Dent wrote:
> I missed this message the first time round. It brings up a possibly
> interesting question. According to the group-theoretical definition of
> meantone, it can equally well be tuned with fifths (say) 1/18 comma
> narrower than pure, as with (say) 1/9 comma.
> > Also according to what Monz says below, about 'tempering out the
> syntonic comma', 1/18-comma should equally well be a member in good
> standing of the meantone class. Making the fifths narrow by 1/18-comma
> is, logically, a way of tempering out 81/80. > > So can a regular tuning with 1/18-comma narrowed fifths possibly be
> meantone, or not, and then why not? (I would argue yes, but only under
> the widest possible meaning of the term.)

You mean because 1/18-comma tempering would mean wider fifths than 12-equal? Yes, that's allowed by some definitions. As would be Pythagorean intonation itself, or negative tempering: fifths sharper than Pythagorean. After all, schismatic temperaments work like this.

So you have to be careful with your definitions.

Graham

9/21/2007 3:58:33 AM

Kraig Grady schrieb:
> well then we don't even need the term scale at all then or mode either . > So melodic integrity of a set of pitches can be consider an impossible > subject. there is nothing in music but harmony . I apologize for being > heavy handed.

No need for sarcasm (I hope). But it's true I think that "scale" is a not-so-important concept unless you are practicing an instrument. Viewed from one angle, it is a subset of the gamut (like the heptatonic white-note scale can be a subset of a chain of fifths, or a periodicity block); the method of construction mostly leads to something larger than a scale. From the melodic side, a scale loses all the information about the roles of notes that a mode entails.

Harmony informs the actual tuning, or rather temperament, since the consensus seems to be that it doesn't like commas.

9/21/2007 12:11:42 PM

--- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, Graham Breed <gbreed@...> wrote:
> Gene Ward Smith wrote:

> > It doesn't really matter. However, starting from a wedgie, you
can
> > construct an appropriate group and mapping directly using the
> > interior product.
>
> It does matter what the group is if you want this to be
> music theory.

A strange pronouncement. What we are mapping from is the group of p-
limit positive rationals (or perhaps some other finite rank subgroup
of the positive reals if we generalize as you want to.) If we assign
a base frequency, say 440 Hz, to the identity, then these are
associated to pitches.

What we are mapping to, the image, can be assciated to quite
different things. For instance, for meantone we could have chains of
fifths separated by octaves. Or, we could have a Bosanquet lattice.
Or, we could use the interior product--in the case of rank two
temperaments, that leads to a subgroup of the vals. These isomophic
groups are concretely quite different, but what they are specifically
does not really matter.

ðŸ”—Graham Breed <gbreed@gmail.com>

That's what you're mapping from, indeed. Although I think they should be interpreted as pitch differences (intervals), not pitches.

Or, we could have a Bosanquet lattice. > Or, we could use the interior product--in the case of rank two > temperaments, that leads to a subgroup of the vals. These isomophic > groups are concretely quite different, but what they are specifically > does not really matter.

Right. So you aren't giving an answer to a musician's question "What is a temperament?" And given an entity in the nearest thing we have to a real world, you can't apply the definition to say whether it's a temperament or not. That means the definition's incomplete.

9/22/2007 12:19:12 AM

Hi Graham;

I believe that after so many years of thinking about it as a musician who also has a formal engineering background,

we have eventually cracked the problem of "conversion" for musicians who wish to think about tuning and harmony in a meantone-type pattern and come to microtuning

"meantone-type" tunings from a 12edo perspective and indocrination.

5L+2s and a unique scalecoding system covers about all that newbies will need to understand to be able to transfer their 12edo musical skills and knowledge to use with meantone-type tunings.

Your more exotic edo's, (e.g. 46, 88 cents , ... ) Miracle, JI, wedgies, orwell etc. are likely to be more difficult for them to adapt to for harmony, counterpoint, scales, chords etc. until we can

come up with similarly simple thought and classification patterns that they will be able to easily grasp.

Charles Lucy lucy@lucytune.com

Charles Lucy wrote:
> Hi Graham;

Hiya!

> I believe that after so many years of thinking about it as a musician > who also has a formal engineering background,
> > we have eventually cracked the problem of "conversion" for musicians > who wish to think about tuning and harmony in a meantone-type pattern > and come to microtuning
> > "meantone-type" tunings from a 12edo perspective and indocrination.

Well, sure, but that means you're converting them from meantone12 to meantone. Nice, but not that useful if you really want to do something different.

(Incidentally, I see you're calling it "meantone". I did propose an MOS naming scheme, which is on the wiki, and 5L+2s is "diatonic". I don't have any terminology storm troopers to enforce it.)

> 5L+2s and a unique scalecoding system covers about all that newbies > will need to understand to be able to transfer their 12edo musical > skills and knowledge to use with meantone-type tunings.

I don't think their skill and knowledge will be so 12edo as all that, which is the point.

> Your more exotic edo's, (e.g. 46, 88 cents , ... ) Miracle, JI, > wedgies, orwell etc. are likely to be more difficult for them to adapt > to for harmony, counterpoint, scales, chords etc. until we can
> > come up with similarly simple thought and classification patterns that > they will be able to easily grasp.

Miracle, orwell, etc, support very similar thought and classification patterns to meantone. You can learn them with exactly the methods you learned meantone. Some temperament classes, like wonder (or whatever it's called now -- 2.3.7-limit 5&31; every other note of miracle) or neutral-thirds diatonics (I called them "mosh" did I?) are inherently easier. Their virtue is also their vice: they're different.

Graham

> > tuning/temperament other than 12-TET?

After that illuminating event Brahms dismissed 12-EDO for ever
even for all his earlier compostions and urged for intonation
as just as possible.
> >
>
> Brahms wrote a lot of choral and orchestral works that have nothing
> necessarily to do with temperament.
His style changed towards JI after his meeting with Tanaka.
>
> The canonical example of JI in Brahms is the final few seconds of the
> Second Symphony, where the three trombones have a D major root
> position chord. I think good brass players will get a pretty much pure
> 4:5:6 there.
Brahms demanded from the brass even sometimes alike Richard Strauss in
http://einfach.jpc.de/jpcng/simple/detail/-/hnum/6083759/rk/simple/rsk/charts
the just pure 11th partial "Alphorn-FA":
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alphorn
"The song describes the time of bringing the cows to the high country
at cheese making time. Rossini introduced the melody into his opera
William Tell. Brahms was clear that the inspiration for the great
melody that opens the last movement of his First Symphony (played in
the orchestra by the horn) was an alphorn melody he heard in the Rigi
area of Switzerland."
That melody contains in melody:
http://www.music.princeton.edu/~ted/alphorn.html
"Very important: the seventh partial, written Bb, middle line treble
clef, is a lowered 7th -- it sounds flat [as it should]. Also, the
Alphorn FA, the 11th partial, written F#, top line treble clef, is a
raised 4th leading to the G [written]: in the key of F# it sounds
in-between B natural and C natural; it is a very distinct sound. These
notes are obviously not out of tune but part of a natural tuning which
western music has trained musicians to think is out of tune! [just
intonation junkies can come back now. -t.]"

There are only few brahms records with properly executed 7/4 septims
-not to mention 11/8 alphorn-fa's-
sufficinet precisely intonated
in matching the harmonic overtone-series pitches correctly,
as originally desired and demanded by the composer himself.

http://findarticles.com/p/articles/mi_m2822/is_2001_Spring-Summer/ai_100808915/pg_12
"Although Alphorns vary in length and therefore the number of pitches
available, most horns produce pitches through the twelfth overtone.
Certain pitches in the harmonic series sound "out of tune" to western
ears (see Ex. 1). In particular, the 11th partial or overtone, often
used in Swiss music and famously known as the "Alphorn-fa" ("fa" being
the fourth pitch of a scale), contributes to the uniqueness and exotic
appeal of mountain music;..."

Conversely inept 12-EDO intonation shoots down Brahms mind dead.

A.S.

that we would not get along in tolerating JI...'

10/13/2007 12:07:10 AM

Hi klaus,

--- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, Klaus Schmirler <KSchmir@...> wrote:

> Does anyone know who claimed that Bruckner introduced the
> fifth of ii as a dissonance after his JI conversion? And
> did anybody ever check if this is true? (Since it would
> only make sense in, say, piano compositions, less in
> orchestral works because of the instruments' flexibility
> or in organ compositions where a retuning can't be
> expected. It does make sense for the harps in the
> symphonies, though, as long as their pedals are kept
> out of play.)

I've read in many sources that Bruckner taught that
the 5th of ii was a dissonance, but AFAIK he *always*
taught that, even before he knew about JI and Tanaka's
53-edo Enharmonium. I remember one time specifically
reading that he taught that, despite the fact that
in 12-edo it wasn't any more true of that interval
than of any of the other 5ths.

If i'm not mistaken, the teaching of this in Austrian
music-theory originated with Simon Sechter, who was
Bruckner's teacher. Sechter published a figured-bass
theory in 1835, and his highly influential
_Die Grundsätze der Musikalischen Komposition_
['the foundations of musical composition'] in 1853-54.
I don't know if the earlier book stated the dissonant
5th of ii theory, but the later one most likely did.
(I haven't read all of it)

The Wikipedia article about him says that Sechter was an
advocate of JI over ET, so that would explain his teaching.

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simon_Sechter

But the demonstration of Tanaka's Enharmonium in 1891
was a real ear-opener for Bruckner. As i wrote in my
"Vienna" webpage:

http://tonalsoft.com/enc/v/vienna.aspx

(BTW, this page appears in various URLs, as Andreas
showed in his post, but this is the only link which
i continue to update.)

>> "In a lecture, Bruckner relates how he used to pass on
>> the dictum of his teachers that 'our ear cannot tolerate
>> pure intonation, and that for this reason tempered
>> tuning was invented', but that he could never again say
>> this after having heard Tanaka's instrument."

But i do think it noteworthy that Joachim had a
Tanaka Enharmonium, because he was indeed a very close
friend of Brahms, and the friendship originated when
they were both young men, so it was not only close but
also lasting. Again from my "Vienna" page, under 1853:

>> "By now an accomplished pianist and composer, [19-year-old]
>> Brahms goes on a tour playing popular 'Hungarian' (really
>> Gypsy) music with 23-year-old violinist Eduard Reményi,
>> and in May meets Reményi's old classmate Joachim, 22, who
>> will be a lifetime friend. When Reményi gets in trouble
>> with the police, Joachim sends both he and Brahms to Liszt."

The next year, it was Joachim who was responsible for
connecting Brahms with Schumann, and perhaps more importantly,
with his wife Clara Wieck Schumann.

-monz
http://tonalsoft.com
Tonescape microtonal music software

ðŸ”—Klaus Schmirler <KSchmir@online.de>

--- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, "Cameron Bobro" <misterbobro@...> wrote:
>
> --- In tuning@yahoogroups.com, Kraig Grady <kraiggrady@> wrote:
> >
> > -Cameron Bobro wrote
> >
> > I feel that the whole basic idea of basing tunings on
> > approximating Just intervals is of dubious artistic integrity.
> > To each their own of course.
> >
> > What would be of artistic integrity?
>
> Using the interval you want, not an approximation of it. Accepting
> a thing for what it is, not what it's "supposed" to be- a taco is
> not a badly made hamburger.

The rub---sometimes people _want_ tempered intervals. Can one blame
them? Why should one blame them? Most temperaments have the property
or tranposability with the same predicitable melodic and harmonic
sound at different pitch levels, which in an awful lot of
circumstances, is a very valuable asset, musically speaking. Ditto not
hearing comma shifts and commatic intervals...sometimes one wants
_subtle_ microtones.

Sorry to bring this topic back--my question would be, does one accept
mistuned JI intervals in JI systems (like 40/27) because they are in
JI systems? If yes, I would wonder why one would accept those and not
similar approximations in systems designed to be practical expediants
to reduce the gamut of notes required to make music.

I believe that the idea the music making can and should be limited to
one tuning system or another is dubious....I really try to look at the
final artwork, and I try not to assign my like or dislike to be based
on the tuning.....just my take on all this.

Best,
Aaron.

>
> The rub---sometimes people _want_ tempered intervals. Can one
>blame
> them?

That's the point- using the interval you actually want, whether
it's tempered, just, or whatever. Don't you see that this does
not jibe with the idea of rating intervals as more or less "good"
or "bad" according to their proximity to Just intervals?

>Why should one blame them?

Who is blaming "them"? I also use tempered intervals, as well as
Just intervals, and intervals which are neither tempered, nor
Just, but follow a different logic- all in the same tuning.
My original comment referred to the "middle path" paper, and
I was referring both to truth-to-materials and to the general
idea of what exactly is it we're aiming for when either choose or
temper intervals? How this gets interpreted as tempered "vs" JI or
extrapolated into some kind of general attack on the "regular
mapping paradigm" is beyond me, for by advocating the idea
of is-what-it-is, I'm actually also talking about what seems to me
to be an excellent potential usage of "regular mapping", mo'
better than using it as a rhinestone factory.

> Sorry to bring this topic back--

You didn't bring the topic back, you brought back whomevers'
knee-jerk interpretation of it. No offense to you intended.

>my question would be, does one >accept
> mistuned JI intervals in JI systems (like 40/27) because they are
>in
> JI systems?

To each their own, as I said in the very first post!

>If yes, I would wonder why one would accept those and >not
> similar approximations in systems designed to be practical
>expediants
> to reduce the gamut of notes required to make music.

Approximation? 40/27 is approximation of what? Lesses, I can enter
intervals into scala without displaying them, what does it sound
like? Very nice! It sounds like a "perfect interval" in character
but it's not a fifth or a fourth... much more "M3"-flavored, and
more beer than wine... okay 680 cents. Sweet. Scala calls it
a "grave fifth", which is a swanky moniker but I fail to find
anything "grave" about something that sounds like a pint of ale to
me- in sound it's not 3/2 with an "accent grave", if anything it
sounds like an extremely broad version of some unidentifiable
interval, like if you mated 5/4 and 4/3 and cornfed their baby.

That's not an "approximation" of jack. 700 cents is
an "approximation" of 3/2.

>
> I believe that the idea the music making can and should be limited
>to
> one tuning system or another is dubious....I really try to look at
the
> final artwork, and I try not to assign my like or dislike to be
based
> on the tuning.....just my take on all this.

IV-I! So you'll agree with me in avoiding rating intervals
as mo' good or mo' bad by virtue of their proximity to JI
intervals.

minor 2nd: 10:9 D 3240
major 2nd: 9:8 D* 3200
Completely identically overtaken into Werckmeister's
"Musikalische Temperatur" FFM 1691 p.31

Werckmeister started at first discussing Ban's JI concept,
that contains the both 2nds tones 9:8-major and 10:9-minor variants.
But the he reduced Ban's 18 tone JI scale down to a 12-tone subset,
in order to 'get rid of all subsemtones or even commata' in his own
new scales:
at the expense of loosing the distinction inbetween 10:9 vs. 9:8.
On p.46 of his "Musicalische Temperatur" he labeled
the arising 'dissonat' 2nd 5th D-A as the ratio from (3:2)(80:81)
clearly as:

"40:27 Quinta commate deficiens"

He considered that any fine discerning inbetween
major and minor tones as useless for the purpose
of compensating by tempering.

on p.60 he concluded his personal observation:
Quote:
"Hieraus ist ja anzunehmen / daÃÂŸ die IMPERFECKten
CONSONANtien das TEMPERAMENT noch besser vertragen kÃ¶nnen
als / als die vollkommenen / und wie weit das GehÃ¶r kÃ¶nne
geteuschet werden / und eine CONSONANS auff gewisse Art vor
eine DISSONANS kÃ¶nne verkauffet werden: Darum hat hier das AXIOMA
PHILOSOHPICUM gute Statt: 'Unius rei plures sunt effectus'.
Wenn aber diese IMPERFECTE CONSONATIEN in der rechten Temperatur
nur ein / und offter nicht ein COMMA schweben / so kann das GehÃ¶r
gar nicht penetrieren / ob sie rein seyn oder nicht / und sind gantz
angenehm. Vom Unterschiede der DISSONANTIEN / weiÃÂŸ offte das GehÃ¶r gar
nichts / ja es weiÃÂŸ nicht TONUM-MAJ. und MIN. zu unterscheiden."
tr:
'From that
(dissonances may represent in tempering consonances and vice-versa)
it can be supposed that imperfect consonants can be tolerated even
much better than the corresponding perfect ones, as far as the keen
eared sense of hearing can be misleaded astray in camouflage.
That allows to sell a consonace for a dissonace in a suspect way.
Therefore here the philosophical axiom may be the case:
'One single reason may cause many diverse effects'

tr:
It is the Joachim-school that represented JI in germany,
conversely to Flesch (12-edo), that drilled more the solistic type.
One can see that in his famous pupils par.ex. Ginette Neveu.
My own first teacher had still studied at J.J. when he was a boy.
He build up the Berlin philharmony and the Concertgebouw-Orchestere
by his own pupils. In germany at that time JI wasn't exotic,
but it was a living practice. Neither JI can be considered as an
"historically" tuning. Without that damn war, it would had stayed
alive. Anyhow Reger still found JI and composed his oevre according
that JI."

Jutta StÃ¼ber's Dissertation "JI in string-playing"
helped her in understanding 19.century JI practice on
violins:
JI was quite common popular and wide used still at Regers time.
Joseph Joachim founded in that JI-tradition the string-classes
at the Berlin conservatory. Over many years recruited
the Berlin Philharmoniker their string players from that resource.

Heutzutage ist unser Ohr so sehr an die dem Klavier angeglichene,
einigermaï¿½en ï¿½temperierte" Stimmung von Orchestern und Ensembles
gewï¿½hnt, dass die meisten Hï¿½rer von heute davon abweichende Stimmungen
wohl als ï¿½unsauberï¿½ bezeichnen wï¿½rden.
--
Today our ears are acoustomed to 12-EDO in pianos so much,
that most coeval listeners may consider deviations from that as
impure.

Die von Frau Eggebrecht verwendete Intonation hat unter anderem zur
Folge, dass durch Berï¿½cksichtigung reiner Quinten und Terzen gewisse
Intervalle weiter, andere enger gegriffen werden.
--
Mrs. Eggebrecht uses JI with the consequence:
5ths & 3rds turn out to be touched pure,
respectively other certain intervals wider or narrower...

Das hat erhebliche Auswirkungen auf die Gestaltung ï¿½ zum Beispiel
kï¿½nnen bei vielen Mehrfachgriffen alle Tï¿½ne lï¿½nger klingen als
herkï¿½mmlich; die gesamte Musik wirkt farbiger und kontrastreicher.
---
That has considerable consequences in form:
Multichords persist longer sounding resonance as in conventional ET,
yielding more living contrast and colourishness in the whole musics.

Die auftretenden Schï¿½rfen sind mithin offenbar von Reger beabsichtigt,
genauso wie beispielsweise Komponisten des frï¿½hen 19. Jahrhunderts
gestopfte Naturtï¿½ne bei Hï¿½rnern als bewuï¿½te Farbe einsetzten.
----
Hence the appearing sharpnesses seem to be fully intened by Reger,
compareable to the natural overtones in horns as concious colour
alike early 19th century composers used them.

Joachim und die Intonation
von: R. Eggebrecht | am: 22.05.2005
Sie haben Recht damit, den Geiger Joseph Joachim im Zusammenhang mit
der reinen harmonischen Stimmung zu nennen. Als Direktor der Kï¿½nigl.
Hochschule fï¿½r Musik machte er Berlin zum Zentrum des
Geigenunterrichts in Deutschland. Sowohl als Solist als auch als
Quartettspieler und Pï¿½dagoge beeinflusste er fast 50 Jahre lang
Generationen von intern. Geigern, die wiederum als hochgeschï¿½tzte
Orchestermusiker (Konzertmeister), Kammermusiker und Solisten
hervortraten. Auch nur einige seiner bekanntesten Schï¿½ler und
Schï¿½lerinnen hier anzufï¿½hren wï¿½rde den Rahmen sprengen, aber
wenigstens Leopold Auer wï¿½re zu nennen, dessen bekannteste Schï¿½ler
wiederum Heifetz, Elman, Milstein, Hansen, Seidel und Zimbalist waren
(Hier kï¿½nnte man nun schon mit einem Buch beginnen).
Wir haben bei unserer Beschï¿½ftigung mit der Kammermusik und dem
Violin-Solo-Werk von Max Reger bemerkt, dass der Komponist diese Werke
vor allem Joachim-Schï¿½lern gewidmet hat. Und siehe da, der griechische
Komponist und Geiger Nikos Skalkottas studierte in Berlin Violine bei
dem Joachim-Schï¿½ler Willy Hess, usw... (wieder ein Buch mï¿½glich!)
Joachim schrieb eine dreibï¿½ndige Violinschule und sein Schï¿½ler, der
Schweizer Geiger Karl Courvoisier beschï¿½ftigte sich in seinem Lehrwerk
ï¿½Die Violin Technikï¿½, 1878, sehr grï¿½ndlich mit der Intonation des
Geigers. Das ï¿½Joachim Quartettï¿½ war weltberï¿½hmt, als Quartettspieler
soll Joachim ï¿½unvergleichlichï¿½ gewesen sein und gerade hier im
mehrstimmigen Spiel hat die reine harmonische Stimmung ihre grï¿½ï¿½te
Bedeutung.

Der bekannte H.v.Helmholtz schrieb, dass Joachim, ï¿½dieser Kï¿½nstler
ersten Ranges, h_ und nicht h als Terz zu g brauchte, e_ und nicht e
als Sexte, und dies nicht nur im Zusammenklang sondern auch im Melosï¿½.
---
Helmholtz wrote:
That fist class artist used h_ -but not h- as 3rd to g,
respectively e_ instead e as 6th, and that not even in harmonics
but also melodically within the single voice.

Wir wissen allerdings, dass auch dieser groï¿½e Geiger Schwierigkeiten
mit der Intonation hatte, wir alle kennen die Geschichte von dem
Verriss, der ihm bei einem Gastspiel in London 1889 fï¿½r seine
Interpretation der D-Dur Solo-Sonate von Bach von dem Kritiker G.B.
Shaw verpasst wurde.
---
But we know also that that great violinist had problems in
intonation. We all know the story of the
roasting scathing scorcher baweld out by his critician G.B. Shaw
on the occasion of his guest-performance London 1889 for his
interpretation of Bach's D-major solo-sonata.

Aber er war weise genug, sich mit dem Problem auseinanderzusetzen und
so kam ihm das 24-tï¿½nige Demonstrationsharmonium in reiner Stimmung
von der Orgelbaufirma E.F. Walcker in Zusammenarbeit mit Shohï¿½ Tanaka
von 1893 gerade recht.
---
But he was wise enough, to grapple with his own intonation problems,
hence in 1893 the Tanaka/Walcker 24-tone JI deomonstration reed-organ
was just the right thing to get rid of that trouble.

Er bestellte es sofort fï¿½r die Berliner Musikhochschule.
---
Immediately he ordered a Tanaka organ for the Berlin conservatory.
(Sadly that instrument went lost by fire in WW2.)

Es wï¿½re fantastisch, wenn heute jede Hochschule so eine Mï¿½glichkeit
der Intonationskontrolle zur Verfï¿½gung stellen wï¿½rde. Das Klavier mit
seiner vï¿½llig unzureichenden "irgendwie" gestimmten Intonation ist fï¿½r
einen bewussten, fundierten Umgang mit der Intonation der Streicher
(Sï¿½nger!) sicher nicht ausreichend.
Mein erster Geigenlehrer, der noch als Knabe Joachim vorgespielt und
spï¿½ter bei Davisson in Leipzig studiert hatte, schulte meine kindliche
Intonation an Duetten. Er spielte immer eine Begleitung zu meinen
Tonleitern und anderen ï¿½bungsstï¿½cken. Das hatte auch der Wiener
Pï¿½dagoge Joseph Bï¿½hm (1795-1876) mit seinem Schï¿½ler Joachim so
gemacht, weil das Duettspiel die "Intonation ungemein fï¿½rdert und
festigt". Auch der Geiger Ferdinand David, von Felix Mendelssohn an

Joachim dieses harmonisch geprï¿½gte Spiel. David's Violinschule enthï¿½lt
gleichfalls viele Duette.

und noch vieles zu sagen wï¿½re -
